Why Divine Will Is Not a Separate Attribute
Logical argument reducing divine will to knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that divine will cannot be a distinct attribute, and why it reduces to knowledge and power.

Core Idea
God's will is not an extra attribute—it is His knowledge and power in action.

This lesson examines a key argument in classical kalam about the nature of divine will. The author contends that if we analyze the concept of will, it either leads to absurdities or collapses into knowledge and power. By following the logical distinctions, we will see why some scholars denied will as a separate attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Familiarize yourself with the Arabic text and its literal translation. Note the logical structure: the author presents two cases for will.
2
Follow the two-case argument
The author divides will into 'prior' and 'concomitant'. For each case, he shows why it is impossible for God. Pay attention to the reasoning for each subcase.
The prior case reduces to 'determination' which requires hesitation. The concomitant case has three subcases, all invalid by prior arguments.
3
Understand the conclusion
After eliminating all alternatives, the only coherent meaning is that will is just God being knowing, powerful, and acting. Hence no separate attribute.
This conclusion is significant in the debate over divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كانت سابقة فهي عزيمة، والعزم لا يتصور إلا في حق من يتردد في شيء ثم أزمع عليه، أو انتهى عن شيء ثم أقبل إليه، وإن كانت مقارنة فهي إما أن تحدث في ذاته أو في محل، أو لا في ذاته ولا في محل والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة بما سبق بطلانها، فتعين أن الإرادة للقديم سبحانه لا معنى لها إلا كونه عالما قادرًا فاعلا.
If it was prior, then it is determination, and determination can only be conceived in one who hesitates about a thing then resolves upon it, or desists from a thing then turns to it. And if it was concomitant, then it is either that it occurs in His essence or in a locus, or neither in His essence nor in a locus. And the three divisions are invalid by what has preceded of their invalidity. So it is established that the will of the Eternal, exalted, has no meaning except His being knowing, powerful, acting.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument aiming to show that divine will cannot be a distinct attribute separate from knowledge and power. The author classifies will into two possible cases: prior or concomitant. If prior, it would be 'determination' ('azm), which requires hesitation—impossible for God. If concomitant, it must either occur in His essence, in a locus, or neither—all shown impossible earlier. Therefore, the only coherent meaning of God's will is that He is knowing, powerful, and acting; will is not an additional attribute.

Key Terms
الإرادة
العزيمة
القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will is a distinct attribute separate from His knowledge and power.
Correction: The author argues that will cannot be a distinct attribute; it reduces to His being knowing, powerful, and acting.

Checkpoint

A) Because determination requires hesitation, which is impossible for God.
B) Because God's will is simultaneous with the act.
C) Because knowledge alone suffices for will.
D) Because power is the same as will.

Key Takeaway
For the author, divine will is not an extra attribute but the expression of God's knowledge and power.

The Distinct Functions of Knowledge, Will, and Power
The author distinguishes the functions of knowledge, will, and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine attributes have distinct functions and how knowledge, will, and power each contribute differently to creation.

Core Idea
Each divine attribute has a unique role: knowledge perfects, will specifies, power brings into existence.

The author responds to an objection that might blur the roles of God's attributes. He warns that if knowledge alone could specify, it could also create, leading to philosophical errors. Instead, each attribute has a distinct function that must be preserved.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the objection and response.
Notice the objection starts with 'قيل له'.
2
Identify the objection and the author's warning
Find where the objector asks whether specification is from knowledge or will. See how the author warns that if knowledge sufficed, it would lead to the philosophers' view.
The author uses a hypothetical: if knowledge could specify, it could also create.
3
Distinguish the three functions
At the end, the author clearly assigns roles: knowledge gives precision and perfection, will gives specification, power gives existence. Note that these are distinct.
Commit this triad to memory: knowledge → ihkam/itqan, will → takhsis, power → ijad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : قد سلمت في الأفعال وجوها من الجواز في تخصيصها ببعض الجائزات دون البعض، فينظر بعد ذلك أهو من دلائل العلم أو من دلائل الإرادة؟ فنقول : قد بينا بأن العلم يتبع المعلوم على ما هو به، سواء كان العلم محيطاً بجميع الوجوه في الفعل وقتًا ومقدارًا وشكلا أو لم يكن، فالعلم من حيث هو علم لا يختلف وإن قدر اختلاف في العلم حتى يكون أحد العلمين مخصصاً دون الثاني، فلنقدر مثل ذلك فيه حتى يكون أحدهما موجدا والثاني غير موجد، ويقع الاجتزاء بالعلم عن القدرة كما وقع به عن الإرادة، فيعود الكلام إلى مذهب الفلاسفة أن علمه تعالى علم فعلي فيوجد من حيث يعلم ويختار الأفضل من حيث يعلم، وإن أن تعطى كل صفة حظها من الحقيقة، فالعلم ما يحصل به الإحكام والإتقان والإرادة ما يحصل بها التخصيص، والقدرة ما يحصل بها الإيجاد والقضايا مختلفة، فالمقتضيات إذا غير متحدة.
It was said to him: You have conceded in the acts aspects of possibility in specifying them with some possible things to the exclusion of others, so consider after that whether it is from the proofs of knowledge or from the proofs of will? So we say: We have shown that knowledge follows the known as it is, whether the knowledge encompasses all aspects of the act in time, amount, and form or not. So knowledge, insofar as it is knowledge, does not differ; and even if difference were supposed in knowledge such that one of the two acts of knowledge specifies to the exclusion of the other, we would suppose something similar in it such that one is existentiating and the other not existentiating; and one would suffice with knowledge instead of power just as one sufficed with it instead of will. Then the discussion returns to the doctrine of the philosophers that His knowledge, exalted, is active knowledge: it brings into existence from where He knows and chooses the best from where He knows. Rather, let each attribute be given its due from reality: knowledge is that by which precision and perfection are obtained, will is that by which specification is obtained, and power is that by which existence is obtained. And the propositions are different, so the requisites are therefore not identical.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and the author's response. The objector concedes that acts have possible aspects and asks whether specification comes from knowledge or will. The author warns that if knowledge suffices for specification, it would also suffice for existence, leading to the philosophers' view of active knowledge. Instead, each attribute has a distinct function: knowledge gives precision (ihkam) and perfection (itqan), will gives specification (takhsis), and power gives existence (ijad). They are not identical, and their requisites differ.

Key Terms
العلم
الإرادة
القدرة
التخصيص
الإحكام
الإتقان
الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge alone can specify which possibilities become actual.
Correction: Knowledge only knows things as they are; specification is the function of will, not knowledge. Attributing specification to knowledge leads to the erroneous view that God's knowledge directly creates.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what does the attribute of will specifically accomplish?
A) Precision and perfection.
B) Existence.
C) Specification.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are not interchangeable: knowledge perfects, will specifies, power creates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Or do you still anxiously rely on your own planning as if you can override His specification? What would change if you lived with the certainty that His knowledge is sufficient and His will is precise?
?

Or do you trust that His knowledge perceives perfection in what you cannot see, His will specifies a greater good, and His power will bring it about in His time? What is one concrete situation where you need to realign your trust in each attribute?

The Ash'ari Doctrine of Eternal Will and Its Relation to Temporal Things
Refutation of objections and exposition of eternal will with attribute hierarchy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that Allah's will is eternal, prior in essence and existence, yet relates to temporal objects in terms of specification, and you will see how this differs from human will and from determination.

Core Idea
Allah's will is eternal, relating to creation without change or hesitation.

The Ash'aris argue that divine will cannot be compared to human will, which arises from incomplete knowledge or desire. In this passage, the author refutes objections against eternal will, clarifies its relationship to temporal events, and distinguishes it from mere determination. You'll also see how knowledge, will, and power are distinct yet hierarchically related.

Guided Path
1
Examine the objection and refutation
Read the opening where the author rejects the analogy from the witnessed world and the division of will into prior or concomitant.
The objector claims will is needed only when the object is not fully encompassed.
2
Learn the Ash'ari position
The author states the Ash'ari doctrine: will is eternal, prior to its object in essence and existence, but concomitant in relation and specification.
This reconciles the eternal attribute with temporal effects.
3
Distinguish will from determination
Understand why eternal will does not imply hesitation or resolve, and how it differs from human psychological states.
Determination involves prior hesitation, which is impossible for God.
4
Map the hierarchy of attributes
Observe the relationships: knowledge follows the actual, power effects without specifying, will specifies. Will follows knowledge; power follows will.
This shows the logical order among divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله : إثبات الإرادة في الشاهد لامتناع الإحاطة بالمراد من كل وجه فباطل؛ لأنا لو فرضنا الإحاطة بالفعل من كل وجه بإخبار صادق أو غيره من الطرق كان يجب أن يستغنى عن الإرادة وليس الأمر كذلك، وتقسيمه القول بأن الإرادة إما سابقة وإما مقارنة. قيل له : قد عرفت على هذا مذهب الصفاتية أن الإرادة أزلية، فهي سابقة على المراد ذاتا ووجوداً، ومقارنة الحال التجدد تعلقا وتخصيصا، والعلم يتبع الواقع ولا يوقع، والقدرة توقع المقدور ولا تخصص والإرادة تخصص الواقع على حسب ما علم بما علم ، والصفة أزلية سابقة، والمراد حادث متأخر، وليس يلزم على ذلك أن يسمى عزماً، فإن العزم توطين النفس بعد تردد، ولو كان المريد للشيء متمنيًا أو مشتهيا أو مائلاً لوجب أن يقال : العالم بالشيء معتقد ساكن النفس مترو ومتفكر، فبطل الاستدلال من هذا الوجه، وليس كل من علم شيئًا إرادة، ولا كل من أراد شيئا قدر عليه، بل كل من فعل شيئًا فقد قدر عليه، ومن قدر على فعل شيء إرادة، ومن أراده علمه، فالإرادة تتبع العلم حتى يتصور أن يكون عالما ولا يكون مريداً ، ولا يتصور أن يكون مريدا ولا يكون عالما.
And as for his saying: The establishment of will in the witnessed world is due to the impossibility of encompassing the object of will from every aspect, that is false; because if we assumed encompassing the act from every aspect by a truthful report or other means, it would be necessary to dispense with will, but that is not the case. And his division saying that will is either prior or concomitant. It was said to him: You have come to know on this the doctrine of the Attribute-holders (as-Sifatiyya) that will is eternal, so it is prior to the object of will in essence and existence, and concomitant with the state of renewal in relation and specification. And knowledge follows the actual and does not effect; power effects the object of power but does not specify; and will specifies the actual according to what is known, with what was known. And the attribute is eternal and prior, and the object of will is temporal and posterior. And it does not follow from that that it be called determination, for determination is the settling of the soul after hesitation. And if the one who wills a thing were hoping, or desiring, or inclined, then it would have to be said that the knower of a thing is a believer, at peace, considering, and reflecting. So the inference is invalid from this aspect. And not everyone who knows a thing wills it, nor does everyone who wills a thing have power over it. Rather, everyone who does a thing has power over it, and whoever has power over an action wills it, and whoever wills it knows it. So will follows knowledge to the extent that it is conceivable that He be knowing without being willing, and it is not conceivable that He be willing without being knowing.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage refutes two points: the witness analogy for will and the division of will into prior and concomitant. First, the analogy fails because even with full encompassing of the act, will is not dispensed with. Then, the Ash'ari doctrine is stated: will is eternal (azaliyya), prior to its object in essence but concurrent in relation and specification. This does not imply determination, which involves hesitation. Will is not desire, hope, or inclination. Finally, the hierarchy is established: will follows knowledge; it is possible to have knowledge without will but not will without knowledge.

الصفاتية
التعلق

العلم
القدرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine will implies hesitation or change in God.
Correction: The passage distinguishes will from determination (عزم), which involves hesitation, and affirms that eternal will relates to temporal events without any change in God's essence or attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position described, how is God's eternal will related to temporal objects?
A) It is temporal and changes.
B) It is prior in essence and existence but relates to temporal objects.
C) It is created at the time of the act.
D) It is identical to knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Allah's will is eternal, relating to temporal events through specification without change or hesitation, and it follows from His knowledge.

?
How does the fact that Allah's will is not like human desire or hope change the way you approach your own desires? Are your desires aligned with what He has willed for you, or are you hoping for something He has not specified? What will you do about that today?

Refutation of an-Najjar's Negative Definition of Will
The author refutes an-Najjar's negative definition of divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining divine will as merely the absence of coercion and impotence is insufficient and how the author refutes it using logical reasoning and examples.

Core Idea
Divine will is a positive attribute, not merely the absence of its opposite.

The theologian an-Najjar defined Allah's will as 'not being overcome and not coerced.' This seems simple, but the author of our text shows why this negative definition fails. Using the example of bodies that lack coercion but lack will, and the example of a person drinking medicine despite aversion, he demonstrates that will must be more than a negation. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand an-Najjar's claim
Read an-Najjar's definition: Allah is willing in the sense that He is not overcome and not coerced.
2
Identify the author's critique
The author argues that this defines a positive attribute (will) by a negation, similar to defining power as not being impotent.
This is the method of the philosophers (mu'attila).
3
First counterexample: inanimate bodies
Many bodies have the negation of impotence and aversion but are not willing. This shows that negation alone does not imply will.
4
Second counterexample: will with aversion
A person drinking medicine despite aversion is willing the medicine while feeling aversion. This proves will can coexist with aversion.
Aversion opposes willingness (taw'), but intention (qasd) can coexist with aversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الرد على النجار حيث قال : إنه مريد بمعنى أنه غير مغلوب ولا مستكره، فيقال له : فسرت حكمًا ثابتا بنفي، كمن يفسر كونه قادرًا بأنه غير عاجز، وكونه عالما بأنه غير جاهل، وذلك مذهب المعطلة الفلاسفة ثم تجرد نفي العجز والكراهية لا يقتضي كون الذات مريدا ، فإن كثيرًا من الأجسام ينفي عنها العجز والكراهية ولا يكون مريدا ، وكثير من المريدين كاره كمن شرب الدواء على كراهية من طبعه وهو مريد له، فالكراهية تضاد الطوع، وأما القصد فقد يجامع الكراهية.
As for the refutation of an-Najjar, where he said: He is willing (murīd) in the sense that He is not overcome and not coerced, it is said to him: You have interpreted a positive judgment by a negation, like one who interprets His being powerful as His not being impotent, and His being knowing as His not being ignorant. And that is the doctrine of the negators, the philosophers. Then the mere negation of impotence and aversion does not necessitate that the essence is willing. For many bodies have the negation of impotence and aversion but are not willing. And many willers are averse, like one who drinks medicine despite aversion from his nature while he is willing it. So aversion opposes willingness, but intention may coexist with aversion.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage refutes an-Najjar's definition of divine will as a negation of being overcome or coerced. The author points out that defining a positive attribute by a negative is like defining power as not being impotent, a method of the philosophers. Moreover, negation of impotence and coercion does not imply will, since inanimate objects also lack these but have no will. The author uses the example of a person drinking medicine despite aversion: he is willing the medicine but has aversion, showing that will and aversion can coexist, so will is not simply the absence of aversion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will is merely the absence of coercion or impotence.
Correction: The author shows that many things lack coercion (e.g., bodies) but have no will, and will can coexist with aversion, so will is not just negation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject an-Najjar's definition of will as 'not overcome nor coerced'?
A) Because it is a negation of a negative.
B) Because many bodies share this negation without being willing.
C) Because it implies change in God.
D) Because it is a philosophical view.

Key Takeaway
Divine will is a positive attribute; defining it by negation is insufficient because negation alone does not entail will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this lesson's distinction between intention and aversion challenge your excuse to give up? Will you commit to acting despite the aversion, knowing your will remains intact?
?

Where in your life are you using the excuse of 'being forced' to avoid responsibility? What would it look like to own your will even under pressure?
?
If defining Allah's will by negation leads to error, what about your own self-definition? Do you define yourself more by what you avoid (sins you don't commit) than by positive acts of obedience? How can you shift from a negative identity to a positive one, actively willing righteousness even when it clashes with your nature?

The Eternal Attribute of Will: Prior and Concomitant
Defense of eternal will as prior and concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school defends the eternal attribute of will as both prior to its objects and concomitant with their temporal occurrence, and how will is distinguished from knowledge and power.

Core Idea
God's will is eternal yet specifies temporal events without change.

This passage presents a key objection to the Ash'ari view of divine will and the school's response. The objector suggests that will could be dispensable and divides it into prior and concomitant. The responder clarifies that will is eternal, prior in essence and concomitant in relation, and then distinguishes will from knowledge and power. Understanding this defense strengthens one's grasp of Ash'ari theology on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
Notice the objection-response structure.
2
Identify the objection and response
Find the objector's claim about will being dispensable and the division. Then see how the responder defends the eternal will as prior and concomitant.
The response refutes the need for abandonment and corrects the division.
3
Analyze the distinctions between knowledge, power, and will
The passage explains how knowledge follows reality, power causes without specifying, and will specifies. Note the logical ordering: action depends on power, power on will, will on knowledge.
This clarifies why will is necessary despite knowledge and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطرق كان يجب أن يستغنى عن الإرادة وليس الأمر كذلك، وتقسيمه القول بأن الإرادة إما سابقة وإما مقارنة. قيل له : قد عرفت على هذا مذهب الصفاتية أن الإرادة أزلية، فهي سابقة على المراد ذاتا ووجوداً، ومقارنة الحال التجدد تعلقا وتخصيصا، والعلم يتبع الواقع ولا يوقع، والقدرة توقع المقدور ولا تخصص والإرادة تخصص الواقع على حسب ما علم بما علم ، والصفة أزلية سابقة، والمراد حادث متأخر، وليس يلزم على ذلك أن يسمى عزماً، فإن العزم توطين النفس بعد تردد، ولو كان المريد للشيء متمنيًا أو مشتهيا أو مائلاً لوجب أن يقال : العالم بالشيء معتقد ساكن النفس مترو ومتفكر، فبطل الاستدلال من هذا الوجه، وليس كل من علم شيئًا إرادة، ولا كل من أراد شيئا قدر عليه، بل كل من فعل شيئًا فقد قدر عليه، ومن قدر على فعل شيء إرادة، ومن أراده علمه، فالإرادة تتبع العلم حتى يتصور أن يكون عالما ولا يكون مريداً ، ولا يتصور أن يكون مريدا ولا يكون عالما.
The ways should have dispensed with will and it is not so, and his division that will is either prior or concomitant. It was said to him: You have known, on this, the school of the Sifatiyya that will is eternal, so it is prior to the object in essence and existence, and concomitant in the state of renewal in relation and specification. Knowledge follows reality and does not cause it, power causes the effect and does not specify, and will specifies reality according to what is known by what is known. The attribute is eternal and prior, and the object is temporal and posterior. It does not follow from that that it is called determination, for determination is settling the soul after hesitation. If the willer of a thing were desirous or aspiring or inclining, it would be necessary to say: the knower of a thing is a believer, at rest, hesitant, and thinking. So the inference is invalid from this aspect. Not everyone who knows a thing wills, nor does everyone who wills a thing have power over it. Rather, everyone who does a thing has power over it, and whoever has power over a thing wills it, and whoever wills it knows it. So will follows knowledge such that it is conceivable that He knows and does not will, but it is not conceivable that He wills and does not know.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response concerning the divine attribute of will (irada). The objector suggests that if the ways (al-turuq) were sufficient, will would be dispensable, and he divides will into prior (sabiqa) and concomitant (muqarina). The responder, speaking for the Ash'ari school (al-Sifatiyya), states that this division is already known: the will is eternal (azaliyya), meaning it is prior to the object (al-murad) in essence (dhatan) and existence (wujudan), yet concomitant with it in terms of relation (ta'alluq) and specification (takhsis) as the object occurs anew. The responder then distinguishes between knowledge ('ilm), power (qudra), and will: knowledge follows reality without causing it, power causes but does not specify, and will specifies reality according to what is known. Since the attribute is eternal and the object temporal, calling it 'azm (determination) is unwarranted because 'azm implies hesitation. Similarly, if willing were merely desire (tamanni) or inclination (mayl), then knowing would also be described as belief, rest, hesitation, and thought, which is false. The conclusion is that the inference is invalid. Finally, the responder clarifies the order: action requires power, power requires will, and will requires knowledge. Will follows knowledge, so it is possible for God to know without willing (some things), but impossible for Him to will without knowing.

سابقة (sabiqa)
مقارنة (muqarina)
أزلية (azaliyya)
مراد (murad)
تعلق (ta'alluq)
تخصيص (takhsis)
عزم ('azm)
علم ('ilm)
قدرة (qudra)
صفاتية (Sifatiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal will implies a change in God because it relates to temporal objects.
Correction: Will is a single eternal attribute that specifies temporal objects without any change in God; it is prior in essence and concomitant in relation.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why is it incorrect to call God's eternal will 'determination' (عزم)?
A) Because determination involves hesitation, which is impossible for God.
B) Because determination is a temporal attribute.
C) Because determination is a type of will.
D) Because determination implies desire.

Key Takeaway
Will is eternal and prior, yet it specifies temporal events; it follows knowledge and precedes power.

Refuting al-Najjar: Will as a Positive Attribute
Author refutes al-Najjar's negative definition of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine will cannot be defined merely as the absence of compulsion and what the true indicator of will is.

Core Idea
Defining will by negation is the method of the Mu'attila; true will is proven by specification of possibilities.

In this passage, the author dismantles al-Najjar's claim that God is willing only in the sense of not being overcome or coerced. He shows that this reduces a positive attribute to a negation, a flawed method akin to the Mu'attila philosophers. The real evidence for will is the specification of actions to particular possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Encounter the Refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the strong opening: 'As for the refutation of al-Najjar...'
2
Identify the Logical Flaw
The author points out that al-Najjar defines a positive judgment (hukm thabit) by a negation (nafy), similar to how the Mu'attila define power as not being incapable.
This is a recurring critique of those who deny positive attributes.
3
Consider the Counterexamples
The author gives two examples: inanimate bodies lack will despite not being compelled, and a person may drink medicine willingly despite aversion.
The medicine example shows that will and aversion can coexist.
4
Focus on the Real Proof
The core evidence for will is the specification (ikhtisas) of actions to some possibilities over others, not the negation of compulsion.
This specification is observable in creation (e.g., choosing this universe over another).
5
Connect to Broader Theology
Understanding this argument reinforces the Ash'ari method of proving attributes through positive proofs (dalail) rather than negations.
It also refutes the Mu'tazilite and Najjari approaches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الرد على النجار حيث قال : إنه مريد بمعنى أنه غير مغلوب ولا مستكره، فيقال له : فسرت حكمًا ثابتا بنفي، كمن يفسر كونه قادرًا بأنه غير عاجز، وكونه عالما بأنه غير جاهل، وذلك مذهب المعطلة الفلاسفة ثم تجرد نفي العجز والكراهية لا يقتضي كون الذات مريدا ، فإن كثيرًا من الأجسام ينفي عنها العجز والكراهية ولا يكون مريدا ، وكثير من المريدين كاره كمن شرب الدواء على كراهية من طبعه وهو مريد له، فالكراهية تضاد الطوع، وأما القصد فقد يجامع الكراهية. ثم نقول : كونه غير مغلوب ولا مستكره أمر مجمع عليه، وإنما الكلام في أنا رأينا في الأفعال ما يدل على كون الصانع مريدا، وهو اختصاص الأفعال ببعض الجائزات دون البعض، وإهمال هذه القضية غير ممكن، فما مدلول هذا الدليل؟ فإن قلتم : مدلوله أنه غير مغلوب ولا مستكره. فيقال : إنما استفدنا العلم بذلك من كونه قادرًا على الكمال والاختصاص لم يدل على القدرة، بل الوقوع دل عليها ، فلم يكن للاختصاص مدلول، ونحن إنما أثبتنا العلم بالصفات من الدلائل
As for the refutation of al-Najjar where he said: He is willing (mureed) in the sense that He is not overcome nor coerced, it is said to him: You have interpreted a positive judgment by negation, like one who interprets His being powerful (qadir) as being not incapable, and His being knowing ('alim) as being not ignorant; and that is the school of the negators (al-mu'attila) the philosophers. Then mere negation of incapacity and aversion does not entail that the essence is willing. For many bodies have incapacity and aversion negated of them and are not willing. And many of the willing are averse, like one who drinks medicine despite the aversion of his nature while he is willing to it. So aversion opposes willingness, but intention (al-qasd) may accompany aversion. Then we say: His being not overcome nor coerced is a matter of consensus. The discussion is only that we have seen in actions what indicates that the Maker is willing, namely the specification of actions to some possibilities without others, and neglecting this issue is not possible. So what is the signification (madlul) of this proof? If you say: Its signification is that He is not overcome nor coerced. It is said: We only derived knowledge of that from His being powerful to perfection, and specification did not indicate power; rather, occurrence indicated it. So specification had no signification. And we only established knowledge of attributes from proofs.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage refutes al-Najjar's definition that God is willing (mureed) merely in the sense of not being overcome (ghayr maghlub) or coerced (ghayr mustakrah). The author argues that this interprets a positive judgment (hukm thabit) by a negation (nafy), which is the method of the Mu'attila (negators) philosophers. Negating incapacity and aversion does not prove that the essence is willing, because many bodies are not described as incapable or averse yet are not willing. Furthermore, the author gives the example of someone drinking medicine despite aversion—he is willing but also averse, showing that aversion does not negate will. After establishing that the consensus is that God is not overcome or coerced, the author focuses on the real issue: we observe in actions a specification (ikhtisas) of some possibilities over others, which indicates that the Maker is willing. The proof is this specification, not the negation of compulsion. If one claims the proof signifies non-compulsion, the author responds that we already know non-compulsion from His power; specification does not indicate power but rather the occurrence of the action indicates power. Thus specification (ikhtisas) is the real indicator of will, not a negation. The passage concludes by affirming that we derive knowledge of attributes from proofs (dalail).

Key Terms
النجار (al-Najjar)
مريد (mureed)
مغلوب (maghlub)
مستكره (mustakrah)
نفي (nafy)
معطلة (Mu'attila)
اختصاص (ikhtisas)
جائزات (ja'izat)
مدلول (madlul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will is merely the absence of compulsion or coercion.
Correction: The negation of compulsion does not prove will; many things are not compelled but lack will. True will is evidenced by specification of possibilities.

Checkpoint
What is the core evidence for God being willing according to the author?
A) He is not overcome or coerced.
B) He is powerful.
C) The specification of actions to particular possibilities.
D) The negation of incapacity.

Key Takeaway
Divine will is proven by the specification of possibilities, not by negation of incapacity or coercion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own will, do you tend to define it by what you are not (e.g., not forced, not coerced) or by the positive choices you make? How does this lesson challenge you to think about God's will differently, and what might you be avoiding by sticking to negations?
?
The author calls al-Najjar's method 'the school of the negators.' Have you ever been tempted to define God's attributes only by what He is not, thinking that protects His transcendence? What would it cost you to instead seek positive proofs for His attributes as the lesson demands?
?
You learned that will is proven by specification of possibilities. Look at your own life: where have you seen God's specification—His choosing one outcome over another—and how does that realization change your trust in His will today?

Proof That God's Will Is Eternal
Proof that God's will is eternal by eliminating temporal possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God's will is eternal by eliminating the possibility of a temporal will.

Core Idea
God's will cannot be temporal because a temporal will would require an impossible locus.

This passage presents a foundational argument for the eternity of God's will. The author uses a disjunctive argument to show that the only rational possibility is that God's will is eternal. We will trace the logical steps and see why temporal alternatives are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the disjunction
Read the passages where the author presents the two options: either God is willing by Himself or by a will. Notice that the argument assumes the latter is established.
The author uses an analogy: 'willing' means 'possessor of will'.
2
Analyze the temporal possibilities
Once it is established that God is willing by a will, that will must be either eternal or temporal. Identify the three temporal subcases mentioned.
The temporal will could be in God's essence, in a locus, or without a locus.
3
Conclude the eternity of the will
Each temporal case is shown to be impossible. Read how the author refutes each, and then conclude that the will must be eternal.
This argument is a classic example of disjunctive syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبتت هذه المسألة أخذنا في المسألة الثانية وهو كون الرب تعالى مريدا بإرادة قديمة. فنقول : قد قام الدليل على أن معنى المريد هو ذو الإرادة، كما قام الدليل على معنى العالم هو ذو العلم، فنقول : إما أن يكون الرب سبحانه مريدا لنفسه أو بإرادة، وإذا تحقق أنه مريد بإرادة، فإما أن تكون الإرادة قديمة وإما أن تكون حادثة ، فإن كانت حادثة فلا يخلو إما أن تكون حادثة في ذاته أو في محل، أو لا في ذاته ولا في محل، وقد سبق الرد على من قال بحدوث الإرادة في ذاته، ويستحيل كون الإرادة في محل ، ويكون الرب تعالى مريداً بها، فإن المعنى إذا قام بمحل وصف المحل به ؛ إذ من المحال وصف الشيئين بمعنى واحد قام بأحدهما دون الثاني، ويستحيل كون الإرادة لا في محل فإن الإرادة من جملة المعاني والأعراض، واحتياج الأعراض إلى المحل صفة ذاتية لها، ومن المحال ثبوتها دون الوصف الذاتي
And when this issue is established, we proceed to the second issue, which is that the Lord, exalted, is willing with an eternal will. We say: Proof has been established that the meaning of 'willing' (al-mureed) is 'possessor of will' (dhu al-irada), just as proof has been established for the meaning of 'knowing' (al-'alim) as 'possessor of knowledge'. We say: Either the Lord, glorified, is willing by Himself or by a will. And if it is verified that He is willing by a will, then either the will is eternal or it is temporal. If it is temporal, then it is either temporal in His essence, or in a locus, or not in His essence nor in a locus. The refutation has already preceded against those who say that will is temporal in His essence. It is impossible for will to be in a locus and for the Lord, exalted, to be willing by it, because if a meaning subsists in a locus, that locus is described by it; it is impossible to describe two things by a single meaning that subsists in one of them without the other. And it is impossible for will to be without a locus, because will is among the meanings (ma'ani) and accidents (a'rad). The need of accidents for a locus is an essential attribute of them, and it is impossible for them to subsist without the essential attribute.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's will is eternal. The author first states that the meaning of 'willing' (mureed) is 'possessor of will' (dhu irada), analogous to 'knowing' meaning 'possessor of knowledge'. Then he presents a disjunction: either God is willing by Himself (li nafsihi) or by a will (bi-irada). Once it is established that He is willing by a will, that will must be either eternal (qadima) or temporal (haditha). The author then eliminates the temporal alternative by considering three possibilities: the temporal will could be in His essence (fi dhatihi), in a locus (fi mahall), or not in His essence nor in a locus. He refers to a previous refutation of will being temporal in His essence (which is impossible because it would imply change). Next, it is impossible for will to be in a locus (i.e., a created thing) because if a meaning subsists in a locus, only that locus is described by it, not another; so if God's will were in another object, God would not be described by it. Finally, it is impossible for will to be without a locus because will is an accident ('arad) and accidents necessarily subsist in a locus. Since all temporal options are impossible, the will must be eternal.

إرادة (irada)
قديمة (qadima)
حادثة (haditha)
ذات (dhat)
محل (mahall)
معاني (ma'ani)
أعراض (a'rad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that God's will could be a temporal accident subsisting in God or in a locus.
Correction: A temporal will would require change in God's essence or would describe not God but the locus, and a temporal will without a locus is impossible because accidents need a locus.

Checkpoint
Why cannot God's will be temporal in a locus (محل)?
A) Because a locus cannot contain a temporal accident.
B) Because if the will subsists in a locus, only that locus is described by it, not God.
C) Because God cannot be described by anything temporal.
D) Because the will is not an accident.

Key Takeaway
God's will must be eternal because any temporal alternative is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is eternal and unchanging, then every event in your life—both joys and hardships—is part of His eternal decree. Are you truly at peace with that, or do you resist His will when it conflicts with your desires? What would it take for you to surrender completely to His will today?
?
The proof shows that God's will cannot be temporal; it is not subject to change or development. Do you live as if God's will is already perfect and settled, or do you sometimes act as if you can change His mind through your efforts? In what area of your life do you need to stop trying to 'convince' God and instead align your will with His?
?
This lesson teaches that God's will is eternal—it always was and always will be. How does this reality confront your tendency to worry about the future or regret the past? Will you trust that His eternal will is better than your own planning, and commit to seeking His pleasure in every decision you make today?

The Proof of Divine Will from Specification
Argument that specification indicates divine will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the specification of acts among possibilities is a proof for God's will (irada) and not merely for His non-coercion.

Core Idea
Specification proves will, not just non-coercion.

This passage deals with a key theological question: what does the observed specification of acts (choosing some possibilities over others) tell us about God? Some say it only shows God is not coerced; the author argues it directly proves that God wills. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the Islamic doctrine of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: an agreed point, the observation of specification, an opponent's claim, and a rebuttal.
The passage is dense but follows a logical argument.
2
Identify the two claims
The author presents two possible interpretations of specification: that it indicates non-coercion, or that it indicates will. Identify which one the author supports and how he refutes the alternative.
3
Trace the rebuttal
The author argues that non-coercion is already known from God's power (qudra), not from specification. Therefore specification would be redundant if it only indicated non-coercion. Follow this logic.
This is the key move in the argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that specification indicates will. Then he transitions to the next issue: the eternity of divine will. Consider how this proof sets up the discussion of eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: كونه غير مغلوب ولا مستكره أمر مجمع عليه، وإنما الكلام في أنا رأينا في الأفعال ما يدل على كون الصانع مريدا، وهو اختصاص الأفعال ببعض الجائزات دون البعض، وإهمال هذه القضية غير ممكن، فما مدلول هذا الدليل؟ فإن قلتم: مدلوله أنه غير مغلوب ولا مستكره. فيقال: إنما استفدنا العلم بذلك من كونه قادرا على الكمال والاختصاص لم يدل على القدرة، بل الوقوع دل عليها، فلم يكن للاختصاص مدلول، ونحن إنما أثبتنا العلم بالصفات من الدلائل، وإذا ثبتت هذه المسألة أخذنا في المسألة الثانية وهو كون الرب تعالى مريدا بإرادة قديمة.
Then we say: His being not overcome nor coerced is a matter agreed upon. Rather, the discussion is that we have seen in actions what indicates that the Maker is willing, and that is the specification of actions to some possibilities over others, and neglecting this issue is not possible. So what is the indication of this proof? If you say: Its indication is that He is not overcome nor coerced. It is said: We only derived knowledge of that from His being powerful to perfection, and specification does not indicate power, rather occurrence indicates it, so specification had no indication. And we only established knowledge of attributes from proofs. And when this issue is established, we proceed to the second issue, which is that the Lord Most High is willing with an eternal will.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a definitional proof for divine will. The author first states that it is agreed that God is not overcome or coerced. The real question is: what does the observed specification of acts to some possibilities rather than others indicate? Some opponents claim it only shows that God is not coerced. The author refutes this: knowledge of non-coercion comes from God's absolute power (qudra), not from specification. Specification itself does not indicate power; rather, the actual occurrence of acts is what indicates power. Therefore, specification would have no distinct role if it only indicated non-coercion. The author concludes that specification indicates will (irada), and once this is established, the discussion moves to the second issue: proving that God's will is eternal.

Misconception: That specification only indicates God is not coerced, not that He wills.
Correction: Non-coercion is already known from God's power, not from specification. Specification has a distinct role: it indicates will.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the real indication of the specification of acts among possibilities?
A) That God is not overcome or coerced.
B) That God is powerful and perfect.
C) That God is willing (mureed).
D) That God is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Specification of acts among possibilities is the proof for God's will, not merely for His non-coercion.

?
The passage argues that specification is not just about God's power but about His active will. Is there an area of your life where you act as if things happen by accident — your career, health, or relationships? Will you consciously affirm today that every specific outcome is willed by God and therefore has purpose?
?
If you acknowledge that God's will is behind every specific occurrence, then your hardships are not meaningless. What is one trial you have attributed to mere chance or fate? Will you now accept it as a deliberate choice from a wise and loving Creator, and how should that change your response to it?

Proof of God's Eternal Will and Refutation of Temporal Will
Sanusi proves God's will is eternal and refutes substrate objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God's will is eternal and why a temporal will cannot be attributed to God without logical contradiction.

Core Idea
God's will must be eternal; a temporal will leads to impossible consequences.

This passage from Niḥāyat al-Aqdām presents a rigorous rational argument for the eternity of God's will. The author systematically eliminates all possibilities for a temporal will, showing that the only coherent position is that God's will is eternal. Along the way, he refutes a subtle objection about the substrate of will, exposing an equivocation in the notion of 'not in a substrate'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the dense logical style typical of kalam.
2
Trace the disjunction
Identify the initial disjunction: God is willing either by Himself or by a will (attribute). Then the will is either eternal or temporal. This sets up the proof.
The author assumes the latter branch (by a will) is correct.
3
Understand the argument against temporal will
The temporal will is examined in three possibilities: in God's essence, in a substrate, or neither. Each is refuted. Focus on the reasoning for why a substrate-less will is impossible (accidents need a substrate).
This is the core of the proof for eternity.
4
Analyze the refutation of the objection
The author anticipates an objection: if a will not in a substrate is conceded, who is the willing? The opponent might say the eternal is more fitting. The author shows equivocation between 'not in a substrate' for God (not in a place) and for will (not in something that occupies space).
This refutation strengthens the necessity of the will being in God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: قد قام الدليل على أن معنى المريد هو ذو الإرادة، كما قام الدليل على معنى العالم هو ذو العلم، فنقول: إما أن يكون الرب سبحانه مريدا لنفسه أو بإرادة، وإذا تحقق أنه مريد بإرادة، فإما أن تكون الإرادة قديمة وإما أن تكون حادثة، فإن كانت حادثة فلا يخلو إما أن تكون حادثة في ذاته أو في محل، أو لا في ذاته ولا في محل، وقد سبق الرد على من قال بحدوث الإرادة في ذاته، ويستحيل كون الإرادة في محل، ويكون الرب تعالى مريدا بها، فإن المعنى إذا قام بمحل وصف المحل به؛ إذ من المحال وصف الشيئين بمعنى واحد قام بأحدهما دون الثاني، ويستحيل كون الإرادة لا في محل فإن الإرادة من جملة المعاني والأعراض، واحتياج الأعراض إلى المحل صفة ذاتية لها، ومن المحال ثبوتها دون الوصف الذاتي، ولو لم تحل في محل لكانت قائمة بذاتها، والقائم بالذات قابل للمعنى، فحينئذ تكون الإرادة قابلة للمعنى، ولا يكون فرق بين حقيقة الجوهر وبين حقيقة العرض حتى لو جوز استغناء العرض عن المحل في وقت من الأوقات جاز في كل وقت، ولو جوز الجنس من الأجناس أو لنوع من الأنواع الجاز لكل نوع وجنس، ولو جوز ذلك أيضا للزم تجويز قيام جوهر بمحل، فإنه كما يستثنى الإرادة والغناء والتعظيم عن جنس من المعاني حتى لا يفتقر إلى محل جاز أن يستثنى جنس من الجوهر حتى يحتاج إلى محل، وكل ذلك محال.

ثم نقول: هب أنا ارتكبنا هذه الاستحالة وتصورنا إرادة لا في محل، فمن المريد بها ومن المعلوم أن نسبتها إلى القديم والحادث على وتيرة واحدة. فإن قيل: يوصف بها القديم؛ لأن القديم لا في محل، والإرادة لا في محل فهي به أولى. قيل: ولو قيل: يوصف بها الحادث لأن الإرادة حادثة والجوهر حادث، والحادث بالحادث أولى، والمناسبات بين الحوادث تتحقق ولا مناسبة بين القديم والحادث بوجه ما، ثم أخذتم قولكم لا في محل بالاشتراك فإن معنى قولنا: القديم لا في محل؛ أي لا في مكان ومعنى قولنا الإرادة لا في محل؛ أي لا في متمكن وفرق بين المكان والمحل فلم توجد المناسبة شاملة للطرفين بمعنى متفق عليه مشترك فيه فلا مناسبة أصلا، هذا كمن يقول: الجوهر لا في محل والإرادة لا في
We say: The proof has been established that the meaning of 'willing' (mureed) is 'possessor of will' (dhu al-irada), just as the proof established that the meaning of 'knowing' (alim) is 'possessor of knowledge'. So we say: Either the Lord, may He be glorified, is willing by Himself or by a will. And if it is established that He is willing by a will, then either the will is eternal (qadeem) or temporal (hadith). If it is temporal, then it cannot avoid being temporal either in His essence (dhat) or in a substrate (mahall), or not in His essence nor in a substrate. And the refutation has preceded against those who say that the will is temporal in His essence. And it is impossible for the will to be in a substrate while the Lord Most High is willing by it, because when a meaning (ma'na) subsists in a substrate, the substrate is described by it; since it is impossible to describe two things by a single meaning that subsists in one of them without the other. And it is impossible for the will to be not in a substrate, because will is among the meanings and accidents (a'rad), and the need of accidents for a substrate is an essential (dhatiyya) attribute of them, and it is impossible for them to be established without the essential attribute. And if it were not in a substrate, it would be self-subsistent (qa'im bi dhatiha), and the self-subsistent is receptive (qabil) to meaning; then the will would be receptive to meaning, and there would be no difference between the reality (haqiqa) of substance (jawhar) and the reality of accident. So much so that if one permitted the independence (istighna') of accident from substrate at one time, it would be permitted at every time. And if one permitted a genus (jins) of genera or a species (naw') of species, it would be permitted for every species and genus. And if one permitted that as well, it would necessitate permitting substance to subsist in a substrate. For just as will, independence (ghina), and exaltation (ta'zim) are exempted (mustathna) from the genus of meanings so that they do not need a substrate, it is permissible to exempt a genus of substance so that it needs a substrate. And all that is impossible. Then we say: Suppose we commit this impossibility and conceive a will not in a substrate, then who is the willing by it? And it is known that its relation (nisba) to the eternal (qadeem) and the temporal (hadith) is uniform. If it is said: The eternal is described by it because the eternal is not in a substrate, and the will is not in a substrate, so it is more fitting (awla). It is said: And if it is said: The temporal is described by it because the will is temporal and the substance is temporal, and the temporal with the temporal is more fitting, and the relations (munasabat) between temporal things are realized, while there is no relation between the eternal and the temporal in any way. Then you have taken your statement 'not in a substrate' by equivocation (ishtirak); for the meaning of our saying 'the eternal is not in a substrate' is that it is not in a place (makan), and the meaning of our saying 'the will is not in a substrate' is that it is not in something that occupies space (mutamakkin), and there is a difference between place (makan) and substrate (mahall). So no comprehensive relation existed for the two sides in an agreed meaning shared between them, so there is no relation at all. This is like someone who says: 'Substance is not in a substrate, and will is not in a substrate...'
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the eternity of God's will, followed by a refutation of a rival objection. The author first establishes that 'willing' means 'possessor of will', analogous to 'knowing' meaning 'possessor of knowledge'. This is a definitional starting point. Then a disjunction: either God is willing by Himself (i.e., His essence is will) or by a separate attribute of will. The author assumes the latter and then considers whether that will is eternal or temporal. The temporal option is examined: a temporal will would have to be either in God's essence (already refuted earlier), or in a substrate (impossible, because then the substrate would be the subject of will, not God), or neither in essence nor substrate (impossible because will is an accident and accidents require a substrate). The argument for the necessary substrate is detailed: if an accident could be substrate-less, it would be self-subsistent and thus receptive to meaning, blurring the distinction between substance and accident. Moreover, it would lead to a slippery slope: if one accident could be independent, all could; if one genus, all genera; and if a substance could be subject to another substance. So the only possibility is that the will is eternal, existing as an attribute in God's essence. The author then anticipates an objection: what if we allow a will not in a substrate? Then the question is: to whom does it belong? The relation of such a will to eternal and temporal is the same. The opponent might argue that the eternal is more fitting because both are 'not in a substrate'. But the author responds by exposing equivocation: 'not in a substrate' for the eternal means 'not in a place', whereas for the will it means 'not in something that occupies space'. Thus there is no real common meaning, so no foundation for the relation. The passage ends with a comparison — someone saying 'substance is not in a substrate and will is not in a substrate' — but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
مريد
إرادة
قديم
حادث
محل
ذات
جوهر
عرض
معنى
غناء
تعظيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a temporally created will can be attributed to God without affecting His essence.
Correction: A temporal will would either be in a substrate (making that substrate the subject) or be self-subsistent (blurring substance/accident), both impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible for God's will to be temporal in a substrate?
A) Because the substrate would then be described by the will, not God.
B) Because a temporal will cannot exist at all.
C) Because will is eternal by definition.
D) Because God cannot have attributes.

Key Takeaway
God's will must be eternal because a temporal will would lead to impossible consequences, such as the will subsisting in a substrate other than God or being self-subsistent and blurring the distinction between substance and accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is eternal and unchangeable, what does that mean for the prayers you make to change your circumstances? Are you truly submitting to His eternal will, or secretly hoping your will can override His? What would it look like today to surrender a specific desire to His eternal decree?
?
The proof shows that a temporal will in God leads to logical absurdities. But do you live as though God's will could change like your own—adjusting to events rather than determining them? When you face difficulty, do you respond with the trust that His eternal will is unalterably wise and good, or do you complain as if events escaped His control?
?
The passage warns against equivocating terms when describing God. Think of one attribute of God you often think of in human terms (like anger, planning, or love). How might you be reducing His reality to your own experience? What concrete step can you take today to purify your understanding of that attribute based on revelation and sound reason?

The Necessity of a Substrate for the Attribute of Will
Proof that will cannot lack a substrate by reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of will cannot exist independently without a substrate, based on the impossibility of collapsing the distinction between substance and accident.

Core Idea
If will were self-subsistent, it would erase the rational distinction between substance and accident.

This passage presents a key argument in Islamic theology concerning the nature of divine attributes. Before we can discuss whether Allah's attributes are eternal or created, we must first understand that even a created attribute like will cannot exist without something to inhere in. Sanusi shows us the logical impossibility of an attribute floating free.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of conditional phrases like 'law' (if) and 'fa' (then).
2
Identify the conditional structure
The argument begins with an assumption: if the will were self-subsistent (qāʾim bi-dhātihā), then it would be receptive to meaning. Identify this conditional and its immediate consequence.
This is a reductio ad absurdum (proof by impossibility).
3
Trace the chain of consequences
From the assumption, the argument draws further consequences: collapsing the distinction between substance and accident, allowing independence for all accidents, and even allowing a substance to depend on a substrate. Follow each step in the text.
The author uses analogy and generalization to show the absurdity.
4
Grasp the conclusion
All these consequences are impossible (muḥāl), so the original assumption must be false. Therefore, the will must subsist in a substrate.
This conclusion reinforces the necessity of a substrate for attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكانت قائمة بذاتها، والقائم بالذات قابل للمعنى، فحينئذ تكون الإرادة قابلة للمعنى، ولا يكون فرق بين حقيقة الجوهر وبين حقيقة العرض حتى لو جوز استغناء العرض عن المحل في وقت من الأوقات جاز في كل وقت، ولو جوز الجنس من الأجناس أو لنوع من الأنواع الجاز لكل نوع وجنس، ولو جوز ذلك أيضا للزم تجويز قيام جوهر بمحل، فإنه كما يستثنى الإرادة والغناء والتعظيم عن جنس من المعاني حتى لا يفتقر إلى محل جاز أن يستثنى جنس من الجوهر حتى يحتاج إلى محل، وكل ذلك محال.
It would be self-subsistent, and the self-subsistent is receptive to meaning; then the will would be receptive to meaning, and there would be no difference between the essence of a substance and the essence of an accident, so that if one allowed the independence of an accident from a substrate at some time, it would be allowed at every time; and if one allowed it for one genus of genera or one species of species, it would be allowed for every species and genus; and if one allowed that, it would also necessitate allowing the subsistence of a substance in a substrate, for just as will, self-sufficiency, and glorification are excepted from the genus of meanings so that they do not need a substrate, it would be allowed to except a genus of substance so that it needs a substrate—and all of that is impossible.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a proof by impossibility (dalīl al-imtināʿ). It argues that if the attribute of will (al-irāda) were not in a substrate (lā fī maḥall), it would be self-subsistent (qāʾim bi-dhātihā). But anything self-subsistent is receptive to meaning (qābil lil-maʿnā). Therefore the will would be receptive to meaning, making it like a substance (jawhar) and erasing the distinction between substance and accident (ʿaraḍ). The argument then extends this by analogy: if an accident were allowed to exist without a substrate in one case, it would be allowed in all cases. Similarly, if one genus of meaning (like will) is excepted from the rule requiring a substrate, then a genus of substance could also be excepted to require a substrate. These consequences are all impossible (muḥāl), so the original assumption is false: the will must subsist in a substrate.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
محل (substrate)
قائم بالذات (self-subsistent)
جوهر (substance)
عرض (accident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an attribute like will can exist independently, without a substrate, like a substance.
Correction: This is impossible because it would destroy the rational distinction between substance and accident, leading to absurd consequences.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if the will were self-subsistent (without a substrate)?
A) It would become a substance.
B) It would be receptive to meaning, collapsing the distinction between substance and accident.
C) It would become an accident.
D) It would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The attribute of will must inhere in a substrate; otherwise it would become like a substance, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage proves that will cannot be self-subsistent; it must inhere in a substrate. When you think about your own will, do you sometimes imagine it as an independent force floating within you? How does this argument challenge or confirm your understanding of your own attributes? Will you reconsider how you view your own will as being anchored to your soul?
?
The argument warns that if we allow an exception for will, then the entire rational structure of substance and accident collapses. Are there areas in your faith where you are tempted to make exceptions for God's attributes that would undermine the logical consistency of tawḥīd? What is one assumption about Allah's attributes that you need to re-examine in light of this reasoning?
?
If the distinction between substance and accident is crucial for understanding the universe, what does that imply about the reality of your own experiences and thoughts? Are you prone to treat some of your attributes (like anger or love) as independent entities that control you, instead of accidents that you are responsible for? How can this lesson help you take ownership of your character?

Refuting the Fitness Argument for Will and Eternal
Objection and its refutation by equivocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that the eternal is more fitting for a substrate-less will by exposing the equivocal use of the term 'without substrate'.

Core Idea
The term 'without a substrate' is used equivocally—it cannot prove fitness.

This passage tackles a sophisticated objection: since both the eternal (God) and the attribute of will are described as 'not in a substrate', perhaps the eternal is the most fitting subject for that will. The author responds with a multi-layered refutation. At its core is the insight that 'not in a substrate' means something different for God (not in a place) than for will (not a locus of inherence). This equivocation dissolves the argument from fitness.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation together. Notice the objection and then the two-part answer.
2
Identify the objection
Find the point where someone claims the eternal is more fitting for the will because both are without substrate.
The objection is signaled by 'فإن قيل'.
3
Trace the first response
The author first counters by suggesting an alternative fitness: the will is originated and the temporal substance is originated, so maybe the temporal is more fitting.
This is a rhetorical parallel, not the main refutation.
4
Understand the main refutation
The author then exposes the equivocation (ishtirāk) in 'without a substrate'. God being 'without a substrate' means not in a place; the will being 'without a substrate' means not inhering in a subject. These are different meanings, so there is no common ground for fitness.
The analogy with substance drives the point home.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: هب أنا ارتكبنا هذه الاستحالة وتصورنا إرادة لا في محل، فمن المريد بها ومن المعلوم أن نسبتها إلى القديم والحادث على وتيرة واحدة. فإن قيل: يوصف بها القديم؛ لأن القديم لا في محل، والإرادة لا في محل فهي به أولى. قيل: ولو قيل: يوصف بها الحادث لأن الإرادة حادثة والجوهر حادث، والحادث بالحادث أولى، والمناسبات بين الحوادث تتحقق ولا مناسبة بين القديم والحادث بوجه ما، ثم أخذتم قولكم لا في محل بالاشتراك فإن معنى قولنا: القديم لا في محل؛ أي لا في مكان ومعنى قولنا الإرادة لا في محل؛ أي لا في متمكن وفرق بين المكان والمحل فلم توجد المناسبة شاملة للطرفين بمعنى متفق عليه مشترك فيه فلا مناسبة أصلا، هذا كمن يقول: الجوهر لا في محل والإرادة لا في محل، فوصف الجوهر بها أولى
Then we say: Suppose we committed this impossibility and conceived of a will not in a substrate, then who is the willing one (al-murīd) by it? And it is known that its relation to the eternal and the originated is of the same mode. For if it is said: The eternal is described by it because the eternal is not in a substrate, and the will is not in a substrate, so it is more fitting for it. It is said: And if it were said: The originated is described by it because the will is originated and the substance is originated, and the originated is more fitting for the originated, and the fitnesses (munāsabāt) between originated things are realized, and there is no fitness between the eternal and the originated in any respect. Then you took your statement 'not in a substrate' equivocally (bi-l-ishtirāk); for the meaning of our statement 'the eternal is not in a substrate' is that it is not in a place (makān), and the meaning of our statement 'the will is not in a substrate' is that it is not in a locus of inherence (mutamakkin). And there is a difference between place and substrate, so fitness was not found between the two parties with an agreed-upon shared meaning—thus there is no fitness at all. This is like someone who says: The substance is not in a substrate and the will is not in a substrate, so describing the substance by it is more fitting.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair. First, the author poses a hypothetical: Even if we grant the impossibility of a will without a substrate and conceive of it, then who is the subject (al-murīd) of that will? The relation of the will to the eternal and to temporal things is the same. An objection is raised: The eternal (qadīm) is more fitting for the will because both are without substrate. The answer: One could alternatively say the temporal is more fitting because both are originated. But the deeper refutation is that the term 'without a substrate' is used equivocally (ishtirāk). When we say the eternal is 'without a substrate', we mean it is not in a place (makān). When we say the will is 'without a substrate', we mean it is not in a locus of inherence (mutamakkin). These are different meanings. Therefore there is no genuine common ground, so the argument from fitness fails. The analogy with substance further illustrates the fallacy.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
محل
مناسبة
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if two things share a description (like 'without substrate'), there is a fitness between them that makes one more appropriate for the other.
Correction: The author shows that the description is used equivocally, so there is no shared meaning that could ground any fitness.

Checkpoint
What is the key point the author uses to refute the claim that the eternal is more fitting for a substrate-less will?
A) The will is originated, so it fits the temporal better.
B) The term 'without a substrate' is used equivocally for God and for will.
C) The eternal cannot have attributes.
D) The will is a substance.

Key Takeaway
The claim that the eternal is more fitting for will because both lack a substrate fails because the key term is used equivocally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own understanding of God's attributes, do you ever assume that because a word is used for both God and creation, it means the same thing? How does this passage force you to reconsider that assumption? What concrete step can you take today to ensure you distinguish between the two when speaking about God?
?
Imagine you are debating someone about God's will. They use an argument very similar to the one refuted here. Would you be able to spot the equivocation? If not, what does that reveal about your own readiness to defend the uniqueness of God? Commit to studying one more example of equivocation in theology this week.
?
The author describes the opponent's mistake as taking the phrase 'not in a substrate' equivocally. Are you ever guilty of taking a word from the Quran or Sunnah and applying it to God in a way that conflates different meanings? How would you even know? What discipline would you need to practice to avoid this error?

6 min
Proof that a temporal will leads to infinite regress
Proof that a temporal will itself requires another will ad infinitum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine will cannot be originated (ḥādith) because it would require another will, leading to an impossible infinite regress.

Core Idea
If the will is originated, it needs another will — leading to an impossible chain.

This passage presents a crisp reductio argument against the idea that God's will could be a temporal (originated) entity. The author shows that the will shares the same condition as all other originated things: it is specified with a time and measure, which implies a specifying agent. But if the will itself needs specifying, we get an infinite regress, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise, logical style of the proof.
2
Identify the general principle
Find the general principle stated at the beginning: every originated thing (ḥādith) is specified with existence and a particular time/measure, implying it is willed (murād).
This principle applies to all originated things.
3
Apply the principle to the will
The will (irāda) itself is an originated thing, so it must also be specified. Therefore it requires another will to specify it.
The will is not exempt from its own rule.
4
Follow the regress
The same reasoning applies to that second will, and so on. This leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is declared false (bāṭil).
Infinite regress is rejected in classical theology.
5
Summarize the argument
Restate the proof in your own words: if the will is originated, it needs another will ad infinitum; since regress is impossible, the will cannot be originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: قد قام الدليل على أن كل حادث اختص بالوجود دون العدم وبوقت وقدر دون وقت وقدر كان المحدث مرادا، والإرادة شاركت جميع الحوادث في هذه القضية، فإنها اختصت بوقت ومقدار من وقت ومقدار فيستدعي إرادة أخرى والكلام فيها كالكلام في هذه، فيجب أن يتسلسل، والقول بالتسلسل باطل.
And it is said: The proof has been established that every originated thing (ḥādith) is specified with existence rather than non-existence and with a time and measure rather than another time and measure, so that the originated thing is willed (murād). And the will (al-irāda) shares with all originated things in this proposition, for it is specified with a time and measure from among times and measures, so it necessitates another will; and the discussion about it is like the discussion about this one, so it must lead to an infinite regress (tasalsul), and the claim of infinite regress is false (bāṭil).
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a proof by reductio ad absurdum. It first states a general principle: every originated thing (ḥādith) is specified with existence over non-existence and with a particular time and measure, which implies that the originated thing is intended (murād) by an agent. The will itself is an originated thing, so it too must be specified with a particular time and measure. Therefore it requires another will that specifies it. The same reasoning applies to that second will, leading to an infinite chain (tasalsul). Infinite regress is rejected as invalid (bāṭil). Hence the original assumption that the will is originated is false.

Key Terms
حادث
إرادة
تسلسل
مريد
مراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that will can be originated without needing another will, because it is the cause of other things.
Correction: The author shows that the will, like any originated thing, is specified and thus requires a specifying cause, leading to regress if it were originated.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of the will being an originated thing (ḥādith)?
A) It becomes eternal.
B) It requires another will to specify it, leading to infinite regress.
C) It becomes a substance.
D) It is self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
If the divine will were originated, it would require another will to specify it, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, do you ever stop to ask what ‘will’ lies behind your own choosing? How does this argument challenge you to see your will as dependent on an eternal will rather than autonomous? What would change in your daily life if you truly lived with that awareness?
?
This passage shows that a temporal will must have a cause. Have you ever felt the weight of your own finite will—its uncertainty and lack of ultimate grounding? How does the idea that your will is not self-sufficient affect the way you approach prayer, repentance, or seeking guidance? Be honest: do you often act as if your will is the final authority?
?
The argument forces a choice: either accept infinite regress or affirm an eternal will. Which have you been implicitly assuming in your life? Is there an area where you have been trying to ground your decisions in an endless chain of human reasoning rather than submitting to the eternal will of Allah? What step can you take today to realign?
